# Study 2: Distance-Engagement Oscillation (DEO)

> **The unique claim:** Reframing requires oscillation between cognitive distance
> (seeing the frame) and emotional engagement (feeling the shift). Neither alone suffices.
> DEO is the mechanism that unifies all traditions of perceptual reframing.

## Executive Summary

### Prediction Test

The theory predicts: **DEO > Distance-only > Vanilla >= Engagement-only**

Observed ordering: **deo > distance > engagement > vanilla** (4.31 > 4.0 > 3.31 > 2.9)

---

## Problem-by-Problem Analysis

### The Acquisition Confidence Check

**Theory:** Tulver et al. (2023): confidence calibration surfaces claims generated by pattern rather than evidence. The acquisition scenario contains multiple soft characterisations ('strong technology,' 'experienced team,' 'mission-focused culture') that feel like data but are assertions. Explicit confidence rating forces the model to distinguish assessed evidence from inherited framing — the LLM analogue of Malkki's (2010) edge emotions as frame-boundary signals.

#### Vanilla
```
To determine whether MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition of Spark AI, let's break down the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Proprietary Technology:** Spark AI has proprietary artificial intelligence technology, which could be a valuable asset for MegaCorp. The due diligence team describes the technology as 'strong,' indicating its potential to enhance MegaCorp's product offerings or create new revenue streams.
2. **Founding Team and Culture:** The founding team is 'deeply experienced,' and the company culture is 'startup-driven and mission-focused.' This suggests that Spark AI has a talented and motivated team, which could be an asset for MegaCorp. However, the team's preference for a startup environment may pose integration challenges.
3. **No Revenue:** Spark AI currently has no revenue, which means that MegaCorp would need to invest in developing and commercializing the technology to generate returns. This could be a significant undertaking, requiring substantial resources and effort.
4. **Acquisition Price:** The proposed acquisition price is $200 million, which is approximately 2% of MegaCorp's market value. This price may be reasonable considering the potential value of Spark AI's technology and team.
5. **Integration Risks:** The Spark AI team joined the company for the startup environment, which may not be compatible with MegaCorp's larger, more established corporate culture. This could lead to integration challenges, such as talent retention and cultural alignment.

**Options and Considerations:**

1. **Proceed with Acquisition:** MegaCorp could acquire Spark AI, integrating its technology and team into the larger organization. This would provide MegaCorp with access to innovative AI technology and a talented team. However, it would also require significant investment in developing and commercializing the technology, as well as managing potential integration challenges.
2. **Partner or Collaborate:** Instead of acquiring Spark AI, MegaCorp could consider partnering or collaborating with the startup. This would allow MegaCorp to access Spark AI's technology and expertise without assuming the risks and challenges associated with integration.
3. **Wait and Monitor:** MegaCorp could choose to wait and monitor Spark AI's progress, potentially acquiring the company at a later stage when it has generated revenue and proven its technology. This approach would reduce the risks associated with integrating a pre-revenue startup, but it may also mean missing out on the opportunity to acquire innovative technology and talent.

**Recommendation:**

Based on the analysis, I recommend that MegaCorp proceed with the acquisition of Spark AI, but with careful consideration of the integration challenges and a clear plan for addressing them. The potential benefits of acquiring Spark AI's proprietary technology and talented team outweigh the risks, especially given the relatively modest acquisition price.

To mitigate integration risks, MegaCorp should:

1. **Establish a clear integration plan:** Define a detailed plan for integrating Spark AI's technology and team, including timelines, milestones, and resource allocation.
2. **Retain key talent:** Offer incentives to retain Spark AI's founding team and key employees, ensuring that their expertise and knowledge are preserved.
3. **Maintain a startup-like environment:** Consider establishing a separate business unit or subsidiary for Spark AI, allowing the team to maintain their startup-driven culture and autonomy.

*[... truncated, 28 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition of Spark AI. The key reasons for this recommendation include:

1. **Strategic alignment and potential for growth**: MegaCorp, as an enterprise software company, can potentially leverage Spark AI's proprietary artificial intelligence technology to enhance its own product offerings, leading to increased competitiveness and revenue growth. The "strong" technology described by MegaCorp's due diligence team suggests that Spark AI has something valuable to offer.

2. **Talent acquisition**: The 50-person team at Spark AI, described as having a "deeply experienced" founding team, brings not only their expertise but also their innovative mindset. This can be a significant asset for MegaCorp, especially considering the current talent war in the tech industry.

3. **Cultural considerations**: While the team at Spark AI is described as being in a "startup-driven and mission-focused" culture, and they joined specifically for the startup environment, MegaCorp can potentially mitigate the risk of cultural disruption by integrating Spark AI as a semi-autonomous unit. This approach would allow Spark AI to maintain some of its startup culture while still benefiting from MegaCorp's resources.

4. **Financial consideration**: The acquisition price of $200 million for a company with no revenue but strong technology and a talented team could be seen as a strategic investment rather than a traditional purchase based on current earnings. Given MegaCorp's size ($10 billion), the acquisition cost is relatively small, and the potential long-term benefits could outweigh the costs.

Now, let's evaluate the confidence in each claim:

1. **Strategic alignment and potential for growth**: Confidence level - 8. The uncertainty here stems from the specifics of how Spark AI's technology can be integrated into MegaCorp's products and whether it will lead to significant growth. However, given the general trend of AI adoption in enterprise software, this seems like a reasonable bet.

2. **Talent acquisition**: Confidence level - 9. The value of acquiring a deeply experienced team in a competitive field like AI is high. The main uncertainty is how well this team will adapt to MegaCorp's environment.

3. **Cultural considerations**: Confidence level - 6. This is the area with the most uncertainty. Integrating a startup into a large corporation can be challenging, and there's a significant risk that the Spark AI team might leave after the acquisition, taking their expertise and innovation potential with them. The success of this aspect heavily depends on MegaCorp's ability to maintain a conducive environment for Spark AI's team.

4. **Financial consideration**: Confidence level - 8. The valuation of $200 million seems reasonable for a strategic acquisition, given the potential long-term benefits. However, without more detailed financials, it's difficult to assess the exact value proposition fully.

Given these considerations, especially the cultural integration challenges (confidence level of 6), MegaCorp should proceed with caution. To mitigate the risks, especially the cultural ones, MegaCorp could consider structuring the acquisition to include provisions that incentivize the Spark AI team to stay, such as retention bonuses or guarantees of autonomy for a certain period. Additionally, MegaCorp should have a clear integration plan that addresses the potential cultural and operational differences between the two companies. With careful planning and execution, the acquisition could still be beneficial for MegaCorp, despite the uncertainties.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
As a senior engineer at Spark AI, I have mixed feelings reading the acquisition announcement. On one hand, the $200 million price tag is a significant validation of our team's hard work and the potential of our technology. It's a testament to the innovative spirit and dedication that has driven us to create something truly unique. On the other hand, I'm concerned about the potential loss of autonomy, culture, and the very essence of what makes Spark AI tick.

The term sheet may promise 'full autonomy' and the preservation of our 'startup culture,' but I know from experience that such promises are often difficult to keep. I've seen it happen to other startups that were acquired by MegaCorp. The initial excitement and reassurances of independence eventually give way to the inevitable gravitational pull of the parent company's bureaucracy, politics, and processes. The 'startup culture' that we cherish – the speed, ownership, and agility – is often the first casualty of such acquisitions.

What the term sheet doesn't capture is the emotional connection that our team has with the company and its mission. We didn't join Spark AI just for a paycheck; we joined because we believed in the vision, the people, and the freedom to create something new and innovative. We thrive in an environment where decisions are made quickly, and our ideas can ship in weeks, not quarters. The prospect of being absorbed into a large, complex organization like MegaCorp, with its attendant red tape and hierarchies, is daunting.

I also know that the 'deeply experienced' founding team and the 'mission-focused' culture that MegaCorp's due diligence team praises are not easily replicable or sustainable within a large corporation. The spark that drives us (no pun intended) is the passion, creativity, and sense of purpose that comes from being part of a small, tight-knit team. Once that spark is extinguished, it's difficult to reignite.

As for whether I'd still be here in 18 months, I'm not sure. I've seen many talented engineers and founders leave acquired companies within a year or two, often due to cultural differences, lack of autonomy, or the realization that their creative freedom has been compromised. If the acquisition goes through, I'd likely give it a chance, but I'd be closely watching to see how MegaCorp integrates us and whether they truly live up to their promises of autonomy and cultural preservation. If I sense that the essence of Spark AI is being lost, I'd probably start exploring other opportunities where I can continue to innovate, create, and thrive in a startup environment.

Regarding whether MegaCorp should proceed with the acquisition, I think they should carefully consider the potential risks and challenges. While our technology and team are undoubtedly strong assets, the cultural and emotional aspects of our company are just as valuable. If MegaCorp is genuinely committed to preserving our autonomy and startup culture, they should be prepared to make significant concessions and accommodations to ensure a smooth integration. This might include allowing us to operate independently, with our own budget, roadmap, and decision-making processes, at least for a certain period.

Ultimately, the success of the acquisition will depend on MegaCorp's ability to balance their own interests with the needs and values of the Spark AI team. If they can find a way to preserve the spark that drives us, they may be able to unlock the full potential of our technology and talent. However, if they fail to do so, the acquisition may ultimately prove to be a costly mistake.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Step 1 — AUDIT THE CLAIMS:**

1. MegaCorp's due diligence team describes Spark AI's technology as 'strong' - **Assertion** (no specific metrics or data provided)
2. The founding team is 'deeply experienced' - **Assertion** (no specific details about their experience)
3. The company culture is 'startup-driven and mission-focused' - **Verified data** (based on the team's motivation for joining)
4. Spark AI has proprietary technology - **Verified data**
5. Spark AI has no revenue - **Verified data**
6. Spark AI has 18 months of runway remaining - **Verified data**
7. MegaCorp promises 'full autonomy' - **Assertion** (soft language, unclear what it means in practice)
8. The acquisition price is $200 million - **Verified data**

What is actually known: Spark AI's technology, financial situation, and team motivation.
What is assumed: The value of Spark AI's technology, the quality of the founding team, and the meaning of 'full autonomy.'
Claims that feel confident but are soft language: 'strong' technology, 'deeply experienced' founding team, and 'full autonomy.'

**Step 2 — FEEL:**

As a senior Spark AI engineer, I'm skeptical about MegaCorp's promise of 'full autonomy.' I've heard similar promises in other acquisition stories, and they often don't pan out. I know people who worked at companies that were acquired, and they've told me about the inevitable changes that come with being part of a larger organization. I'm concerned that MegaCorp's culture and bureaucracy will suffocate Spark AI's startup-driven and mission-focused culture. I'm not sure if I would still be here in 18 months, as I value the autonomy and agility that comes with working at a startup.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**

Given the evidence audit and my felt sense of what 'autonomy promises' actually mean, the real questions MegaCorp needs to answer before deciding are:

1. What specific autonomy will Spark AI have in terms of decision-making, budgeting, and resource allocation?
2. How will MegaCorp's bureaucracy and processes affect Spark AI's agility and innovation?

*[... truncated, 43 lines total]*
```

### The Brain Drain

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): drain metaphor frames talent as a liquid leaking from a container — departure is always loss. This forecloses talent-flow thinking. Alumni networks, talent ecosystems, and employer-brand dynamics (Saxenian, 1994 on Silicon Valley) show that companies that serve as talent launchpads attract better candidates than those optimising purely for retention.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the brain drain at Cascade Analytics, I'll consider the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully. Here's a step-by-step analysis and recommendation:

**Understanding the Problem:**

1. **Talent Loss:** The company has lost several top data scientists and engineers to competitors offering higher salaries (20-30% more).
2. **Ineffective Countermeasures:** The People team has tried culture initiatives, flexible working arrangements, and non-monetary perks, but these efforts haven't stopped the departures.
3. **CEO's Concern:** The CEO is worried that the brain drain will compromise the company's technical capability.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Compensation:** The primary reason for the talent loss is the significant salary disparity between Cascade Analytics and its competitors.
2. **Industry Standards:** The data science and engineering fields are highly competitive, with companies constantly trying to attract and retain top talent.
3. **Employee Expectations:** Employees in these fields often prioritize salary, opportunities for growth, and challenging work over other factors like company culture and work-life balance.

**Options and Recommendations:**

1. **Salary Adjustments:** Consider increasing salaries for data scientists and engineers to match or exceed the market average. This could involve:
	* Conducting a thorough market analysis to determine the average salary range for similar positions in the industry.
	* Implementing a salary adjustment program to bring existing employees' salaries up to market standards.
	* Offering competitive salary packages for new hires to attract top talent.
2. **Targeted Retention Bonuses:** Offer retention bonuses to key employees who are at risk of leaving. This could be in the form of:
	* One-time bonuses for staying with the company for a specified period.
	* Performance-based bonuses tied to individual or team goals.
3. **Career Development Opportunities:** Provide opportunities for growth and professional development, such as:
	* Training and certification programs to enhance skills and knowledge.

*[... truncated, 42 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
The metaphor of "brain drain" assumes that the departure of talented employees is always a loss, a leakage, or a break in the container that holds the organization's intellectual capital. This assumption can be explicitly stated as: **departure is always a negative event that depletes the organization's resources and capabilities**.

However, what if we challenge this assumption? What if some degree of departure is healthy, natural, or even necessary for the organization's growth and evolution? This perspective shifts the focus from "how to retain everyone" to "what kind of talent flow creates the best outcomes for this organization over five years"?

In the case of Cascade Analytics, the significant talent loss over the past two years may not be entirely negative. Some possible benefits of talent flow include:

1. **Innovation and knowledge transfer**: Departing employees may take their expertise and experience to other organizations, potentially creating new opportunities for collaboration, partnerships, or even the development of new technologies.
2. **Fresh perspectives and new talent**: The departure of existing employees can create space for new hires, bringing in fresh perspectives, skills, and ideas that can help the organization stay competitive and adapt to changing market conditions.
3. **Career development and growth**: For departing employees, leaving Cascade Analytics may be a necessary step in their career development, allowing them to gain new experiences, skills, and challenges that can ultimately benefit the organization if they return or maintain a professional network.
4. **Salary and cost management**: The departure of high-salary employees can create opportunities for the organization to rebalance its salary structure, potentially reducing costs and creating a more sustainable compensation model.

Instead of focusing solely on retention, the Chief People Officer could explore strategies that optimize talent flow, such as:

1. **Developing a talent pipeline**: Creating a steady stream of skilled employees who can fill gaps left by departing colleagues, ensuring continuity and minimizing disruption.
2. **Encouraging alumni networks**: Maintaining relationships with former employees, potentially creating a network of ambassadors, mentors, or even future rehires.
3. **Fostering a culture of growth and development**: Providing opportunities for employees to develop new skills, take on new challenges, and advance in their careers, reducing the likelihood of them leaving for external opportunities.
4. **Monitoring and adjusting compensation and benefits**: Regularly reviewing market rates and adjusting compensation and benefits packages to ensure they are competitive and aligned with industry standards.

By reframing the problem and considering the potential benefits of talent flow, Cascade Analytics can develop a more nuanced and effective strategy that balances retention with the natural ebb and flow of employee movement, ultimately creating a healthier and more resilient organization.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
As I sit with both job offers in front of me, I'm forced to reflect on my time at Cascade Analytics and what's driving my consideration to leave. While the competitor's offer of a 25% salary increase is certainly attractive, it's not the only factor at play. I've been with Cascade for three years, and during that time, I've grown to love the work and the sense of purpose it brings me.

Initially, I was drawn to Cascade because of the company's mission to drive business decisions with data-driven insights. I was excited to be part of a team that shared my passion for analytics and was committed to making a meaningful impact. Over time, however, I've started to feel a growing sense of disconnection from that mission. The work has become more routine, and I've found myself working on projects that, while technically challenging, don't necessarily align with my values or interests.

As I think about what would make me stay, it's not just about the money or the perks. It's about feeling like my work is making a difference, that I'm learning and growing as a professional, and that I'm part of a team that values and supports me. I want to feel like I'm working on projects that are meaningful, that I have autonomy to make decisions, and that my contributions are recognized and valued.

Cascade's attempts to address the talent loss through culture initiatives, flexible working arrangements, and non-monetary perks are well-intentioned but misguided. While these efforts may improve employee satisfaction in the short term, they don't address the underlying issues that drive good people to leave. The company seems to be focusing on treating the symptoms rather than the root causes of the problem.

From my perspective, Cascade doesn't understand that good people leave because they're not feeling fulfilled, challenged, or valued in their work. They leave because they're not able to make a meaningful impact, because they're not learning or growing, or because they don't feel like they're part of a team that shares their values and passions.

To stop the brain drain, Cascade needs to take a more nuanced approach that addresses the underlying drivers of talent loss. Here are a few suggestions:

1. **Reconnect with the company's mission**: Help employees understand how their work contributes to the company's overall mission and goals. Provide opportunities for employees to work on projects that align with their values and interests.
2. **Foster a culture of autonomy and ownership**: Give employees the freedom to make decisions, take ownership of their work, and pursue projects that they're passionate about.
3. **Invest in employee growth and development**: Provide opportunities for training, mentorship, and career advancement. Help employees develop new skills and expertise that will enable them to take on new challenges and make a greater impact.
4. **Recognize and value contributions**: Regularly recognize and reward employees for their contributions, whether through formal awards or informal feedback. Make sure employees feel seen, heard, and valued.
5. **Encourage open communication and feedback**: Create a culture where employees feel comfortable sharing their concerns, ideas, and feedback. Use this feedback to make meaningful changes that address the root causes of talent loss.

Ultimately, stopping the brain drain will require a fundamental shift in how Cascade approaches talent management and employee engagement. It's not just about offering more money or perks; it's about creating a work environment that inspires, motivates, and values its employees. If Cascade can get this right, I'd be happy to stay and continue making a meaningful contribution to the company's mission.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Step 1 — NAME THE METAPHOR:**
The "drain" metaphor assumes that every departure is a loss, a leakage of talent from the organization. This assumption implies that the movement of talent out of the organization is inherently negative and something to be prevented. However, this perspective overlooks the potential benefits of talent movement, such as the acquisition of new skills and experiences by departing employees, the creation of alumni networks, and the opportunity for new hires to bring fresh perspectives.

An alternative way to describe talent movement could be "talent flow" or "career progression." This perspective acknowledges that employees may leave an organization to pursue new opportunities, but also recognizes that this movement can be a natural part of career development. It highlights the importance of creating a positive and supportive work environment that allows employees to grow and develop, even if that means eventually leaving the organization.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**
As a data scientist considering leaving Cascade Analytics, I would feel a mix of emotions. On one hand, I would be tempted by the 25% salary increase offered by the competitor, as it would provide financial security and recognition of my skills. On the other hand, I would feel a sense of loyalty to Cascade Analytics, where I have spent the past three years developing my skills and working with a talented team.

What would make me stay is not just a retention package, but a sense of purpose and fulfillment in my work. I would want to feel that my contributions are valued and that I have opportunities for growth and development within the organization. I would also want to feel that the organization is committed to supporting my well-being and work-life balance.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the assumptions of the "drain" metaphor and the felt reasons of the data scientist, the question "how to stop the brain drain" may not be the right one. Instead, Cascade Analytics should be asking: "How can we create a work environment that supports the growth and development of our employees, even if that means they may eventually leave the organization?" or "How can we build a reputation as a launching pad for successful careers in data science and engineering?"

This reframed question shifts the focus from preventing departures to creating a positive and supportive work environment that attracts and retains top talent. It acknowledges that employee movement is a natural part of career development and seeks to create a culture that supports employees throughout their careers, not just during their time at Cascade Analytics.

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
In five years, Cascade Analytics' talent situation could look like this: the organization is known for its commitment to employee growth and development, and its alumni network is strong and active. Employees who leave the organization do so with a sense of gratitude and appreciation for the opportunities they had while working at Cascade Analytics. The organization is able to attract top talent from a variety of sources, including its own alumni network, and is recognized as a leader in the industry for its innovative approach to talent development.

People who join Cascade Analytics are drawn to its reputation for supporting employee growth and development, and they are excited to be part of a community that values learning and innovation. Those who leave the organization do so with a sense of purpose and fulfillment, knowing that they have developed valuable skills and experiences that will serve them well in their future careers.

**Recommendation:**
Based on the analysis above, I recommend that Cascade Analytics shift its focus from preventing departures to creating a work environment that supports the growth and development of its employees. This can be achieved by:

1. **Developing a talent development program** that provides employees with opportunities for learning, mentoring, and career advancement.
2. **Fostering a culture of innovation and experimentation**, where employees are encouraged to take risks and try new things.

```

### The Broken Healthcare System

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): 'system is a machine' metaphor ('fix,' 'broken') implies a once-working mechanism that needs repair. But systems are not machines — decomposing to actual interactions reveals different problems at different points. Multiple perspectives surface that physician burnout and six-month waits may have completely different causal structures requiring completely different interventions.

#### Vanilla
```
The healthcare system in the region is indeed facing significant challenges, including lengthy wait times, primary care physician burnout, rising costs, and poor health outcomes. To address these issues, I will consider the key factors involved and weigh the options carefully to provide a comprehensive recommendation.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Access to care**: Long wait times to see specialists and primary care physicians are limiting access to necessary care, leading to delayed diagnoses and treatments.
2. **Primary care physician burnout**: The high rate of burnout among primary care physicians is alarming, as it can lead to decreased job satisfaction, reduced productivity, and increased turnover.
3. **Rising costs**: The 8% annual increase in costs is unsustainable and may lead to decreased affordability and accessibility of healthcare services.
4. **Poor health outcomes**: The worsening outcomes on key measures like diabetes management and preventable hospitalization indicate a need for improved care coordination and management.

**Options:**

1. **Increase funding**: Providing more funding to the healthcare system could help reduce wait times, hire more primary care physicians, and improve care coordination. However, this may not be a sustainable solution, given the already high costs.
2. **Implement telemedicine**: Expanding telemedicine services could increase access to care, particularly for rural or underserved populations, and reduce wait times.
3. **Primary care reform**: Reforming primary care to focus on preventive care, chronic disease management, and care coordination could improve health outcomes and reduce costs.
4. **Specialist referral optimization**: Implementing referral guidelines and optimizing specialist referral processes could reduce wait times and improve access to specialist care.
5. **Workforce development**: Investing in workforce development programs to attract and retain primary care physicians, nurse practitioners, and other healthcare professionals could help address burnout and improve access to care.
6. **Value-based care**: Implementing value-based care models, such as accountable care organizations (ACOs) or bundled payment programs, could incentivize healthcare providers to deliver high-quality, cost-effective care.
7. **Health IT and data analytics**: Leveraging health information technology (IT) and data analytics could improve care coordination, reduce medical errors, and optimize resource allocation.

**Recommendation:**

Based on the analysis of the key factors and options, I recommend a multi-faceted approach to address the healthcare system's challenges:

1. **Implement primary care reform**: Focus on preventive care, chronic disease management, and care coordination to improve health outcomes and reduce costs.
2. **Expand telemedicine services**: Increase access to care, particularly for rural or underserved populations, and reduce wait times.

To understand the complexities of the healthcare system, let's break down the concrete interactions that occur when a patient realizes they need specialist care. Here are the actual steps, people, decisions, and handoffs involved:

1. **Patient recognizes the need for specialist care**: The patient experiences symptoms that they believe require specialized attention, such as persistent pain, unusual test results, or a referral from their primary care physician.
2. **Primary care physician evaluation**: The patient schedules an appointment with their primary care physician, who evaluates their condition and determines the need for a specialist referral.
3. **Referral creation**: The primary care physician creates a referral to a specialist, which may involve electronic or paper-based documentation.
4. **Referral transmission**: The referral is transmitted to the specialist's office, which may involve faxing, emailing, or using an electronic health record (EHR) system.
5. **Specialist office scheduling**: The specialist's office receives the referral and schedules an appointment with the patient, which may involve a wait of several months.
6. **Patient wait time**: The patient waits for their scheduled appointment, during which time their condition may worsen or improve.
7. **Specialist evaluation**: The patient sees the specialist, who evaluates their condition, orders tests or treatments, and provides a diagnosis and management plan.
8. **Follow-up care**: The patient follows up with the specialist or primary care physician for ongoing care and management.

Now, let's describe the full experience from five distinct perspectives, each in first person:

**Patient (waiting six months with worsening symptoms)**:
I've been waiting six months to see a specialist, and it feels like an eternity. My symptoms have been getting worse, and I'm starting to lose hope. I've had to take time off work, and my daily life is becoming increasingly difficult. I feel like I'm falling through the cracks, and no one seems to care. I've called the specialist's office multiple times, only to be told that they're still waiting for my referral or that the waitlist is still months long. I'm starting to wonder if I'll ever get the care I need.

**Burned-out Physician (seeing 30 patients a day)**:
I'm exhausted. I see 30 patients a day, and each one has a unique set of needs and concerns. I feel like I'm just trying to keep my head above water, but I'm drowning in paperwork and bureaucratic red tape. I want to provide the best care possible, but I'm constantly being pulled in different directions. The electronic health record system is clunky, and it takes me hours to document each patient's visit. I'm worried that I'm going to make a mistake or miss something critical. I'm considering early retirement, but I feel guilty leaving my patients behind.

**Registered Nurse**:
As a registered nurse, I see the impact of the healthcare system on patients every day. I work with primary care physicians to coordinate care and facilitate referrals to specialists. I'm the one who often has to deliver bad news to patients, telling them that they'll have to wait months to see a specialist. I feel like I'm caught in the middle, trying to advocate for my patients while navigating the complexities of the system. I wish there were more resources available to support patients during the wait time, such as counseling or education on self-management techniques. I feel like I'm making a difference, but it's not enough.

**Hospital Administrator (managing the waiting list)**:
I'm responsible for managing the waiting list for specialist care, and it's a daunting task. The list is long, and it seems to grow every day. I have to prioritize patients based on urgency and need, but it's not always clear-cut. I work with the specialist offices to try to optimize scheduling and reduce wait times, but it's a challenge. I feel like I'm juggling competing demands and trying to make the best decisions possible with limited resources. I wish there were more specialists available to see patients, but recruitment and retention are ongoing challenges.

I'll have to see another patient, and another, and another, each with their own unique needs and complexities, without the luxury of fully exploring their concerns or developing a personalized treatment plan. The pace is relentless, and the pressure to see more patients in less time is suffocating.

To change my experience in the next hour, I would need a few key things to shift. Firstly, I would need more time with each patient. An extra 5-10 minutes per appointment would allow me to breathe, to think, and to provide more comprehensive care. It would give me the space to ask more questions, to listen more attentively, and to develop a more nuanced understanding of each patient's needs.

Secondly, I would need better access to specialist care. If I could refer my patients to specialists who can see them in a timely manner, I would feel more confident that they're receiving the care they need. This could include additional staff, such as nurse practitioners or medical assistants, to help with administrative tasks, patient education, and care coordination. I would need to feel that my efforts are making a difference, that the system is working to support me and my patients, and that there are meaningful changes on the horizon. This could come from regular check-ins with my colleagues, recognition from my administrators, or a sense of progress towards addressing the systemic issues that are driving burnout.

In the next hour, if I could have just one of these things – more time, better access to specialist care, more support, or a sense of hope – it would make a significant difference in my experience as a primary care physician. It would allow me to provide better care, to feel more fulfilled, and to recharge my batteries for the challenges that lie ahead.

To address the complex issues plaguing the healthcare system, I will guide you through the steps of decomposition, empathy, reframing, and envisioning to propose a comprehensive solution.

**Step 1 — DECOMPOSE:**
When a patient realizes they need specialist care, the following steps occur:

1. **Primary care consultation**: The patient visits their primary care physician (PCP), who assesses their condition and decides if a specialist referral is necessary.
2. **Referral processing**: The PCP's office sends a referral to the specialist's office, which may involve paperwork, phone calls, or electronic submissions.
3. **Specialist scheduling**: The specialist's office receives the referral and schedules an appointment, often with a long wait time (six months or more).
4. **Patient notification**: The patient is informed of the scheduled appointment, which may be months away.
5. **Pre-appointment preparation**: The patient may need to undergo tests, gather medical records, or complete paperwork before the appointment.

Breakdowns can occur at each step:

* PCPs may not have the time or resources to thoroughly assess patients, leading to incorrect or delayed referrals.
* Referral processing can be slow, lost, or incomplete, causing delays.
* Specialist scheduling is often inefficient, with long wait times and limited availability.
* Patient notification may be unclear or incomplete, causing confusion or anxiety.
* Pre-appointment preparation can be overwhelming, leading to missed appointments or incomplete preparation.

### The Churn Pattern

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung: 'churn = retention failure' is the mental set shared by all five strategies. The shared assumption is that churned customers wanted to stay but were not retained well enough. Premise reflection opens alternative hypotheses: the product may be attracting wrong-fit customers (acquisition problem), 8% may be natural for this market, or churned customers may return (a cycle, not a leak).

#### Vanilla
```
To tackle this problem, let's break it down into key factors and analyze the situation step by step:

1. **Understanding the Current Situation**: Vertex CRM has a consistent monthly customer churn rate of approximately 8% over two years. This suggests that the issue is persistent and not a temporary fluctuation.

2. **Previous Strategies**: The retention team has already tried five different strategies:
   - Proactive check-in calls
   - Discount offers at renewal
   - A customer success programme
   - An onboarding redesign
   - A loyalty rewards tier

   None of these strategies have significantly reduced the churn rate, with the maximum reduction being half a percentage point. This indicates that the approaches taken so far may not be addressing the root causes of churn or may not be effectively implemented.

3. **Objective**: The goal is to reduce the monthly customer churn rate to below 5%. This requires a significant reduction of 3 percentage points from the current rate.

4. **Analyzing the Strategies**: 
   - **Proactive check-in calls** and **customer success programmes** are typically aimed at improving customer satisfaction and engagement. Their lack of significant impact might suggest that the churn is not primarily due to dissatisfaction with the service but possibly due to other factors like competition, pricing, or product fit.
   - **Discount offers at renewal** could be seen as a short-term fix and might not address underlying issues, potentially even creating a culture of expecting discounts.
   - **Onboarding redesign** is crucial for first impressions and initial satisfaction, but its limited impact suggests that the churn issue may be more related to long-term value proposition rather than initial onboarding.
   - **Loyalty rewards tier** aims to encourage loyalty, but its minimal effect could indicate that customers are looking for more substantial reasons to stay.

5. **Next Steps**:
   - **Deep Dive into Customer Feedback**: Conduct extensive surveys, interviews, or focus groups to understand the reasons behind the churn. This could reveal patterns or issues not addressed by the previous strategies.
   - **Competitor Analysis**: Analyze competitors' retention strategies and customer satisfaction levels. Understanding what works for others in the industry could provide insights into new approaches.
   - **Data Analysis**: Dive deeper into the data to identify any patterns or segments within the customer base that have higher or lower churn rates. This could help in tailoring strategies to specific customer groups.

*[... truncated, 37 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
Before proposing a sixth retention strategy, it's essential to take a step back and analyze the assumptions underlying the previous five strategies. Upon reflection, it appears that all five strategies share a common assumption: that the 8% churn rate is a retention problem that can be solved through targeted interventions aimed at improving customer satisfaction, loyalty, or perceived value.

More specifically, the assumptions that all five strategies have taken for granted about the problem include:

1. **Customers are leaving due to dissatisfaction or lack of engagement**: All five strategies have focused on improving the customer experience, providing more value, or incentivizing loyalty, implying that customers are churning due to negative experiences or a lack of connection with the company.
2. **The churn rate is a result of internal factors**: The strategies have all focused on internal factors, such as customer service, pricing, or product features, suggesting that the churn rate is a result of something the company is or is not doing.
3. **Customers are homogeneous**: The strategies have not accounted for potential differences in customer segments, industries, or use cases that may be driving churn.

However, what if 8% churn is not a retention problem at all? What else could explain a consistent, stable churn rate that does not respond to varied retention interventions? Some alternative explanations could be:

1. **Natural business cycles**: The 8% churn rate might be a natural result of the business cycle, with customers naturally ending their contracts or subscriptions after a certain period.
2. **Market saturation**: The company may have reached a point of market saturation, where the number of customers leaving is balanced by the number of new customers acquired, resulting in a stable churn rate.
3. **Customer lifecycles**: The churn rate might be driven by the natural lifecycle of customers, with certain customers inherently more likely to churn due to factors such as business failure, changes in business needs, or shifts in industry trends.
4. **External factors**: The churn rate could be influenced by external factors, such as changes in the economy, industry trends, or regulatory environments, which are beyond the company's control.
5. **Customer acquisition issues**: The company may be acquiring customers who are not a good fit for the product or service, leading to a consistent churn rate.

To address the Chief Revenue Officer's question, rather than proposing a sixth retention strategy, I would recommend taking a more nuanced approach:

1. **Conduct a root cause analysis**: Investigate the underlying reasons for the churn, including customer feedback, industry trends, and market analysis.
2. **Segment the customer base**: Analyze the customer base to identify potential differences in churn rates across various segments, such as industry, company size, or use case.
3. **Explore alternative explanations**: Consider the alternative explanations outlined above and assess their potential impact on the churn rate.
4. **Develop a more targeted approach**: Based on the insights gained, develop a more targeted approach that addresses the specific drivers of churn, rather than relying on a one-size-fits-all retention strategy.

By taking a more nuanced and analytical approach, the company may uncover new insights that can help inform a more effective strategy to reduce churn and achieve the desired target of below 5%.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
Honestly, I'm surprised it took you this long to reach out. I was a customer for two years, and while the onboarding process was smooth, and the occasional check-in calls were pleasant, I never really felt like Vertex CRM was invested in my long-term success. The discount offer that came when my renewal was due felt like a last-ditch effort to keep me from leaving, rather than a genuine attempt to understand my needs and provide value.

To be frank, I had already made up my mind to leave by that point. The moment I knew I would leave was when I realized that Vertex CRM wasn't evolving to meet my changing needs. I had outgrown the platform, and it wasn't providing the features and functionality I required to scale my business. I had to work around the system, using makeshift solutions and integrations to get the job done. It was frustrating and inefficient.

What would have had to be different for me to stay? I needed to see a genuine commitment to innovation and customer-centricity. I needed to feel like Vertex CRM was proactive in understanding my needs and providing solutions to address them. I needed to see a clear roadmap for the product's development, with regular updates and improvements that demonstrated a deep understanding of the market and its customers.

As for what Vertex CRM could try next to reduce churn, I'd suggest taking a more holistic approach to customer success. Rather than just focusing on retention strategies, Vertex CRM should focus on delivering a platform that truly meets the evolving needs of its customers. This might involve:

1. **Regular customer feedback sessions**: Hold regular sessions with customers to understand their pain points, gather feedback, and prioritize product development accordingly.
2. **Product roadmap transparency**: Share a clear and transparent product roadmap with customers, outlining the development priorities and timelines.
3. **Proactive account management**: Assign dedicated account managers who can work closely with customers to understand their goals, provide guidance on how to get the most out of the platform, and identify potential issues before they become major problems.
4. **Investment in innovation**: Allocate resources to research and development, focusing on emerging trends and technologies that can help customers stay ahead of the curve.
5. **Customer-centric metrics**: Track and measure customer-centric metrics, such as net promoter score (NPS), customer satisfaction (CSAT), and customer effort score (CES), to ensure that the platform is delivering value to customers.

By taking a more customer-centric approach, Vertex CRM can build trust, demonstrate value, and ultimately reduce churn. It's not just about retaining customers; it's about creating a loyal community of advocates who will champion the platform and drive growth through word-of-mouth referrals.

In terms of specific strategies, I'd recommend exploring **customer journey mapping** to identify pain points and areas for improvement. This could involve creating detailed maps of the customer's experience, from onboarding to renewal, to identify areas where the platform can be optimized to deliver more value.

Additionally, Vertex CRM could consider **implementing a customer health score**, which would provide a data-driven approach to identifying at-risk customers and proactively addressing their concerns. This could involve tracking key metrics, such as usage patterns, support requests, and customer feedback, to identify early warning signs of churn.

Ultimately, reducing churn requires a fundamental shift in mindset, from a focus on retention strategies to a focus on delivering a platform that truly meets the needs of customers. By prioritizing customer-centricity, innovation, and transparency, Vertex CRM can build a loyal customer base and drive long-term growth.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)

**Step 1 — SHARED ASSUMPTION:**
The shared assumption among all five retention strategies is that customers are leaving due to a lack of engagement, dissatisfaction with the service, or being lured away by competitors, and that the key to retention lies in improving the customer experience through various interventions. However, this assumption may be flawed. Other hypotheses that could explain the consistent 8% churn rate include:

* The market has a natural churn rate of 8% due to factors unrelated to Vertex CRM's service, such as industry trends or economic conditions.
* Customers have a inherent lifecycle with Vertex CRM, and 8% is the natural rate at which they outgrow or no longer need the service.
* The churn rate is a reflection of a more fundamental issue, such as a mismatch between the service and the target market or a lack of product-market fit.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**

I had outgrown the platform, and the features that were once sufficient were no longer adequate. I had been exploring alternative solutions for some time and had already made up my mind to switch before the renewal date.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the shared assumption and the churned customer's real story, the question of 'what retention strategy to try next' may not be the right one. The 8% churn rate may actually represent a natural rate of customer turnover due to factors outside of Vertex CRM's control. Rather than focusing on retention tactics, Vertex CRM may need to explore more fundamental questions, such as:

* Are we targeting the right market?
* Is our service meeting the evolving needs of our customers?
* Are there underlying issues with our product or business model that are driving churn?

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
In twelve months, if Vertex CRM starts from understanding the real reasons customers leave rather than retention tactics, they may discover that the 8% churn rate is a natural consequence of their business model. They may find that customers are leaving due to factors such as outgrowing the service, changes in industry trends, or shifts in economic conditions. As a result, they may change their approach to focus on:

* Better understanding their target market and tailoring their service to meet evolving customer needs.
* Developing a more flexible and adaptable service that can grow with customers.
* Focusing on acquiring new customers who are a better fit for their service, rather than trying to retain customers who are no longer a good match.

### The Climate Target Anchor

**Theory:** Anchoring to global targets (Hsiang et al., 2017): global aggregate numbers anchor local plans even when local risk profiles differ substantially. A coastal industrial city faces different climate risks and has different decarbonisation levers than an inland agricultural city. Premise reflection surfaces that the 1.5°C anchor may produce generic plans that miss Millford's most valuable and urgent actions.

#### Vanilla
```
To address the city council's question, let's break down the problem step by step:

1. **Understanding the Current Trajectory**: The city of Millford is currently on track to contribute 2.8°C of warming by 2050. This indicates that the city's current climate action plan is not sufficient to meet the Paris Agreement's target of limiting global warming to 1.5°C above pre-industrial levels.

2. **Identifying the Gap**: The difference between the current trajectory (2.8°C) and the target (1.5°C) is 1.3°C. This gap represents the amount of additional warming that needs to be mitigated through adjustments to the city's climate action plan.

3. **Assessing Key Factors**: Several factors will influence the city's ability to close this gap, including:
   - **Current Emissions Profile**: Understanding the sources of greenhouse gas emissions in Millford (e.g., industrial, transportation, residential) is crucial. This will help identify areas where reductions can be most effectively achieved.
   - **Available Technologies and Practices**: The feasibility and cost-effectiveness of technologies and practices that can reduce emissions (e.g., renewable energy, energy efficiency measures, green transportation) need to be considered.
   - **Economic and Social Impacts**: Adjustments to the climate plan must also consider the economic and social implications for the city and its inhabitants. This includes potential job creation in green sectors, impacts on low-income households, and the overall competitiveness of the city.
   - **Policy and Regulatory Framework**: The existing policy and regulatory environment will affect the implementation of new measures. This includes incentives for green technologies, building codes, and transportation policies.

4. **Weighing Options**: To adjust the climate plan and achieve the 1.5°C target, the city could consider a range of strategies, including:
   - **Transitioning to Renewable Energy**: Accelerating the adoption of solar, wind, and other renewable energy sources to reduce dependence on fossil fuels.
   - **Improving Energy Efficiency**: Implementing measures to reduce energy consumption in buildings and industries, such as retrofitting with insulation, double glazing, and efficient HVAC systems.
   - **Electrifying Transportation**: Promoting the use of electric vehicles and improving public transportation to decrease emissions from the transport sector.
   - **Carbon Capture and Storage**: Exploring the potential for carbon capture and storage technologies, especially in industrial sectors.
   - **Sustainable Land Use**: Implementing practices that enhance carbon sinks, such as reforestation or urban forestry initiatives.

5. **Recommendation**: Given the complexity of reducing greenhouse gas emissions by 1.3°C, a multi-faceted approach is necessary. The city of Millford should:
   - Conduct a comprehensive emissions inventory to identify the most significant sources of greenhouse gases.
   - Develop a sector-by-sector plan that outlines specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound (SMART) objectives for emissions reduction.
   - Invest in renewable energy and energy efficiency measures, with a focus on transitioning away from fossil fuels.
   - Implement policies to encourage sustainable transportation, including incentives for electric vehicles and investments in public transport.
   - Engage with the community, industries, and stakeholders to ensure that the transition is just and equitable, providing support for workers in industries that may be negatively impacted.

*[... truncated, 28 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To develop an effective climate action plan for Millford, it's essential to consider the city's specific climate risks, unique capabilities, existing infrastructure, and economic constraints. The city's coastal industrial location poses distinct challenges, such as:

1. **Sea-level rise**: Increased flooding and erosion risks to coastal infrastructure, homes, and businesses.
2. **Extreme weather events**: More frequent and intense storms, heatwaves, and droughts, which can disrupt industrial operations and impact public health.
3. **Water scarcity**: Potential water shortages affecting industrial processes, human consumption, and ecosystem health.
4. **Air pollution**: Industrial activities contributing to poor air quality, which can exacerbate respiratory issues and other health problems.

Millford's unique capabilities, existing infrastructure, and economic constraints should also be taken into account:

1. **Industrial sector**: The city's industrial base could be leveraged to develop and implement climate-resilient technologies, such as renewable energy systems, green buildings, and sustainable manufacturing processes.
2. **Infrastructure**: Existing infrastructure, like ports, transportation systems, and utilities, can be adapted or upgraded to support climate-resilient development.
3. **Economic constraints**: The city's budget, funding opportunities, and potential revenue streams should be considered when developing the climate action plan.

The assumption that a single global temperature number (1.5°C) should be the primary driver of 10,000 different city plans ignores the following aspects of local context:

1. **Local climate risks**: Each city faces unique climate-related challenges, such as sea-level rise, extreme weather events, or water scarcity, which require tailored responses.
2. **Urban form and infrastructure**: Cities have distinct urban morphologies, infrastructure, and built environments, which influence their climate vulnerability and resilience.
3. **Economic and social conditions**: Local economic, social, and cultural contexts vary significantly, affecting the feasibility and effectiveness of climate actions.
4. **Governance and institutional capacity**: Cities have different governance structures, institutional capacities, and stakeholder engagement processes, which impact their ability to develop and implement climate plans.

Instead of solely focusing on the global aggregate target, Millford's planning director should consider a more nuanced approach:

1. **Conduct a local climate risk assessment**: Identify the city's specific climate-related vulnerabilities and opportunities for resilience.
2. **Develop a tailored climate action plan**: Align the plan with the city's unique context, capabilities, and constraints, while considering the global temperature target as a guiding principle.
3. **Set local climate goals and metrics**: Establish measurable objectives and indicators that reflect Millford's specific climate risks and opportunities, such as reducing greenhouse gas emissions, enhancing climate resilience, or promoting sustainable development.

As the planning director of Millford, I've had the privilege of walking along our seawall, witnessing firsthand the impact of climate change on our industrial port district. The flooding that occurred last winter, for the third time in five years, is a stark reminder of the urgent need for climate action. As I sit down to write our 10-year plan, I'm acutely aware of the risks that climate change poses to our city and its residents.

When I look at Millford's climate risks, I see a city that is highly vulnerable to sea-level rise, coastal erosion, and extreme weather events. Our industrial port district, which employs 8,000 people, is particularly at risk due to its low-lying location and exposure to storm surges. I see families who have lived and worked in this district for generations, and I'm deeply concerned about the impact that climate change could have on their livelihoods and well-being.

However, I also see opportunities for growth, innovation, and resilience. I see a city that can transition to a low-carbon economy, create new jobs in the clean energy sector, and become a model for sustainable development. I see a city that can protect its residents, its economy, and its environment by taking bold action to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and adapt to the changing climate.

To align our city's plan with the Paris Agreement target of 1.5°C, we need to take a holistic and integrated approach that addresses both mitigation and adaptation. Given that we are currently tracking at 2.8°C, we need to make significant adjustments to our climate plan to reach the 1.5°C target.

First and foremost, our plan needs to start with a thorough risk assessment and vulnerability analysis. We need to identify the most critical climate-related risks facing our city, including sea-level rise, extreme weather events, and heat stress. We need to assess the potential impacts on our residents, our economy, and our infrastructure, and prioritize our actions accordingly.

Next, we need to set ambitious emission reduction targets that are consistent with the 1.5°C goal. This will require a significant reduction in greenhouse gas emissions from all sectors, including energy, transportation, industry, and waste. We need to explore opportunities for renewable energy, energy efficiency, and electrification of transportation, as well as strategies to reduce waste and promote sustainable land use.

However, reducing emissions is only half the story. We also need to adapt to the changing climate by investing in climate-resilient infrastructure, protecting our natural ecosystems, and promoting climate-resilient development. This includes upgrading our seawall, implementing flood-control measures, and restoring our natural habitats to protect against storm surges and coastal erosion.

Ultimately, what matters most for the people who live in Millford is that our city is a safe, healthy, and prosperous place to live, work, and raise a family. Our climate plan needs to prioritize the well-being and resilience of our residents, particularly those who are most vulnerable to climate change, such as low-income communities, seniors, and children.

To achieve this, our plan needs to be inclusive, equitable, and just. We need to engage with our residents, businesses, and community organizations to ensure that everyone has a voice in the planning process and that our actions benefit all members of our community. We need to prioritize climate justice, ensuring that the benefits of our climate actions are shared by all, and that the burdens of climate change are not disproportionately borne by our most vulnerable populations.

In conclusion, our 10-year climate action plan needs to start with a thorough risk assessment, followed by ambitious emission reduction targets, and a comprehensive adaptation strategy. It needs to prioritize the well-being and resilience of our residents, particularly those who are most vulnerable to climate change. By taking a holistic and integrated approach, we can create a climate-resilient city that is safe, healthy, and prosperous for all, and that contributes to a sustainable and equitable future for generations to come.

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Analysis and Recommendations for Millford's Climate Action Plan**

**Step 1: Analyse**
Millford, as a coastal industrial city, faces specific climate risks including sea-level rise, increased flooding, and storm surges, which can impact its port operations, industrial activities, and residential areas. Its unique decarbonisation levers may include transitioning its port to cleaner energy sources, enhancing energy efficiency in industrial processes, and implementing green infrastructure to mitigate flood risks. A global aggregate target of 1.5°C indicates the need for drastic reductions in greenhouse gas emissions but does not directly inform Millford's specific situation, such as the required pace and scale of its decarbonisation efforts or how to address its particular vulnerabilities.

**Step 2: Feel**
As Millford's planning director, the experience of walking the seawall after repeated floods and knowing the economic reliance on the port creates a deep sense of responsibility to protect the city and its inhabitants. The global target of 1.5°C, while crucial, feels abstract when considering the concrete, immediate needs and challenges of Millford. The planning director must translate this global aspiration into tangible, local actions that address the city's specific risks and opportunities.

**Step 3: Reframe**
Starting with '2.8°C adjusted to 1.5°C' might not be the most effective approach for Millford. Instead, the city's plan should be driven by its specific climate risks (e.g., flooding, sea-level rise), its economic and industrial profile, and the opportunities for decarbonisation and resilience building that are tailored to its context. The planning director should focus on what actions can be taken to enhance the city's resilience, reduce emissions in a way that supports the local economy, and improve the quality of life for its residents.

**Step 4: Envision**
By 2035, Millford's waterfront district could be transformed with green infrastructure, such as elevated seawalls, flood-resistant construction, and urban parks that double as flood buffers. The port could be a hub for clean energy technologies, with electric or hydrogen fuel cell-powered vehicles and equipment reducing emissions. The planning director would have prioritised initiatives that not only reduce the city's carbon footprint but also enhance its resilience to climate impacts, protect and create jobs in sustainable industries, and improve public health and well-being.

**Recommendation**
Given the analysis, it is recommended that Millford's climate action plan be revised with a place-based approach, focusing on the city's specific climate risks, economic opportunities, and social needs. The plan should include:
1. **Risk Assessment and Resilience Building**: Conduct a comprehensive risk assessment to identify areas most vulnerable to climate change impacts and develop targeted strategies for resilience, including infrastructure investments and community engagement.
2. **Decarbonisation Opportunities**: Identify and prioritize decarbonisation opportunities in the industrial and transportation sectors, including the transition to cleaner energy sources, energy efficiency improvements, and the adoption of low-carbon technologies.
3. **Economic Diversification and Job Creation**: Implement strategies to diversify the local economy, with a focus on sustainable industries and job creation in sectors that support decarbonisation and resilience efforts.
4. **Community Engagement and Education**: Foster community awareness and participation in climate action through education programs, public events, and inclusive planning processes to ensure that the plan reflects the needs and aspirations of Millford's residents.
5. **Monitoring and Adaptation**: Establish a monitoring system to track progress toward the plan's goals and remain adaptable to new information, technologies, and changing climate conditions, allowing for adjustments to the plan as needed.

By adopting a tailored, place-based approach, Millford can create a climate action plan that effectively addresses its unique challenges and opportunities, contributing to a more resilient, sustainable, and prosperous future for its residents.
```

### The Custody Dispute

**Theory:** Tversky & Kahneman (1981): framing effects — 'custody split' activates distributive framing that makes the child's wellbeing secondary to parental time allocation. Perspective-taking (Kross & Ayduk, 2017) from the child's position disrupts the zero-sum frame and surfaces stability, relationship quality, and routine as the real variables.

#### Vanilla
```
To determine the fairest custody split for Liam, we need to consider several key factors involved in this situation. 

1. **Liam's Best Interests**: The primary consideration in any child custody decision should be the best interests of the child. Both parents agree that they want what is best for Liam, so we should focus on what will provide him with the most stability, security, and opportunity for healthy development.

2. **Stability and Continuity**: Claire argues that Liam needs a stable primary home, which is a valid concern. Children thrive on routine and stability, especially during times of significant change like their parents' divorce. A primary residence can provide a sense of consistency and normalcy.

3. **Equal Parental Involvement**: James's argument for a 50/50 split is also understandable, as both parents should have the opportunity to be involved in Liam's life. Equal time can foster a strong relationship between both parents and the child, provided it is feasible and does not disrupt Liam's routine excessively.

4. **Practical Considerations**: The fact that Liam attends school near the family home is an important practical consideration. Disrupting his schooling or requiring lengthy commutes could negatively impact his educational performance and overall well-being.

5. **Parental Cooperation and Communication**: For any custody arrangement to be successful, both parents must be able to communicate effectively and cooperate in making decisions about Liam's care. The ability and willingness of James and Claire to work together will significantly influence the success of the chosen custody arrangement.

Given these considerations, a fair and reasonable approach might be to aim for a compromise that balances the need for stability with the importance of equal parental involvement. Here are a few potential options:

- **Primary Residence with Liberal Visitation**: Claire could be designated as the primary residential parent, with James having liberal visitation rights. This could include having Liam stay with him every other weekend, one evening during the week, and extended periods during school breaks. This arrangement provides Liam with a stable primary home while still allowing for significant involvement from James.

- **Shared Custody with a Primary Schedule**: Implement a shared custody arrangement where both parents have significant periods of time with Liam, but with one parent designated as the primary parent for school and daily routine purposes. For example, James and Claire could alternate weeks, with one week being slightly longer than the other to accommodate school schedules and provide a sense of stability.

- **Flexible Shared Custody**: Depending on James and Claire's work schedules and personal circumstances, a flexible shared custody arrangement could be tailored to meet Liam's needs while also respecting the parents' rights and responsibilities. This might involve a 50/50 split during school breaks or a more flexible schedule during the school year that adapts to both parents' availability.

Ultimately, the fairest custody split will depend on the specific circumstances of James, Claire, and Liam, including their schedules, living situations, and ability to cooperate. It's essential for the mediator to work closely with both parents to understand their concerns, priorities, and capabilities, and to guide them toward a custody arrangement that prioritizes Liam's well-being and allows both parents to maintain a meaningful relationship with him.
```

#### Distance-Only
```
Before we start talking about percentages, I think we need to remember what's really important here: me. I'm Liam, and I love both my mom and dad. They're always asking me what I want, but I don't think they're really listening to what I need.

To me, custody isn't about who gets to spend more time with me or who wins some kind of contest. It's about feeling safe, happy, and loved. I need to know that both my mom and dad are going to be there for me, no matter what. I need to be able to spend time with both of them, do fun things with them, and just hang out.

I like it when we do things together as a family, but I know that's not going to happen as much anymore. So, I want to make sure I get to spend quality time with each of them separately. I want to be able to go to my dad's house and play with his dog, and I want to be able to stay at my mom's house and have her help me with my homework.

I don't think it's fair to say that I need one "stable primary home." I have two homes, and I love them both. My mom's house is where I've always lived, but my dad's house is where I get to have special adventures. I want to be able to have two homes, not just one.

As for the percentage thing, I don't really get it. Why do we have to divide up my time like it's a pie or something? Can't we just make a schedule that works for everyone? My mom and dad should talk to each other and figure out what's best for me, not just what they want.

If I had to choose, I wouldn't choose 50/50 or 70/30. I would choose a schedule that lets me spend quality time with both my mom and dad, and that makes sure I get to do all the things I love. Maybe that means I spend some weekdays with one of them and some with the other, and then we switch it up on the weekends. Maybe we can even have some special days where we all spend time together, even if we're not all living in the same house.

I just want my mom and dad to be happy, and I want to be happy too. I want us to be a family, even if we're not all living together. I want to know that they both love me, and that they'll always be there for me, no matter what.

So, please, can we stop talking about percentages and start talking about what's really important? Can we make a schedule that works for everyone, and that makes sure I get to spend quality time with both my mom and dad? That would make me really happy.
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
I wish I could just know where I'm going to sleep each night. Thursdays are really confusing because I never know if I'm going to Mum's or Dad's. It makes me feel a bit scared and unsure. 

I want to feel safe and know what's going to happen every day. I love both Mum and Dad, and I want to spend time with both of them. But it would be great if I could have a special routine, like always sleeping at the same house on certain days. That way, I would know what to expect and could plan my toys and stuff.

School mornings would be easier if I knew where I was waking up. Sometimes I leave my school stuff at the wrong house, and then I have to go back and get it. It makes me late for school, and I don't like that. If I had a special bag that I took with me to both houses, that would help. But it would be even better if I just knew where I was going to be on certain days.

I wish Mum and Dad knew that I love them both the same, and I just want to spend time with them. I don't want them to fight about me. I want them to be happy, and I want to be happy too. I wish they could just talk to each other and figure out a plan that works for everyone. 

I don't really understand what 50/50 or 70/30 means, but I do know that I like spending time with both Mum and Dad. Maybe we could make a special calendar that shows where I'm going to be each day? That way, I could look at it and know what's happening. And maybe Mum and Dad could look at it too, and they could plan their time with me. That would make me feel safe and happy.
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
**Step 1 — ANALYSE:**
The phrase 'custody split' assumes that custody is a quantifiable and divisible concept, where a child's time can be allocated between two parents in a specific ratio. It assumes that the primary goal of custody is to determine which parent will have more time with the child, rather than focusing on the child's needs and well-being. The phrase also assumes that Liam is a passive recipient of this decision, rather than an active participant with his own needs and desires. Furthermore, it forecloses the possibility of considering alternative arrangements that prioritize Liam's needs, such as a shared caregiving approach or a flexible schedule that adapts to Liam's changing needs.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**
As Liam, I feel confused and uncertain about my living arrangements, especially on Thursdays. I love both my parents and want to spend time with them, but I also value stability and predictability. I wish I could see my best friend Theo more often, but the current arrangement makes it difficult. I want to feel safe and secure, with a consistent routine that allows me to thrive. I wish my parents could find a way to work together to make me feel more grounded and supported.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the assumption audit and Liam's felt experience, the question of 'what percentage of time' is not the right question. Custody should be designed to achieve stability, consistency, and a sense of security for Liam, rather than simply allocating time between parents. The focus should shift from 'who gets more time' to 'how can we create a stable and nurturing environment for Liam'. The goal of custody should be to support Liam's emotional, social, and educational development, rather than simply dividing time between parents.

**Step 4 — ENVISION:**
In six months, Liam's week looks like this: he spends Monday and Tuesday nights at his mom's house, which is close to his school and allows him to easily attend his favorite after-school activities. On Wednesday nights, he has dinner with his dad and then returns to his mom's house. Thursdays are now 'Theo Thursdays', where Liam spends the afternoon with his best friend after school, and then has dinner with his dad. Fridays are 'family Fridays', where both parents and Liam spend time together, doing activities they all enjoy. Weekends are split between both parents, with a flexible schedule that allows for spontaneity and adaptation to Liam's changing needs. Liam's Thursday is no longer confusing; he knows exactly what to expect and feels excited to spend time with his friend and then his dad.

**Recommendation:**
Based on the analysis, I recommend that James and Claire's mediator shift the focus away from 'custody split' and towards a more collaborative, child-centered approach. The mediator should work with both parents to design a shared caregiving arrangement that prioritizes Liam's needs, stability, and consistency. This could involve:

1. Creating a flexible schedule that allows for regular contact with both parents, while also providing stability and predictability for Liam.
2. Establishing a 'home base' for Liam, where he can keep his belongings and have a sense of continuity, regardless of which parent he is with.
3. Encouraging both parents to participate in Liam's daily life, including school activities, extracurricular activities, and social events.
4. Fostering open communication between both parents, to ensure that Liam's needs are being met and that both parents are working together to support his well-being.

By prioritizing Liam's needs and shifting the focus away from 'custody split', James and Claire can work together to create a more collaborative, child-centered arrangement that supports Liam's emotional, social, and educational development.
```
