### The War on Competition

**Theory:** Lakoff & Johnson (1980): argument-as-war metaphor constrains available moves to attack, defend, counterattack, withdraw. Jazz analogy (structurally distant) surfaces co-creation, audience differentiation, and complementarity as the operative logic. CompetitorX may have validated and expanded the market rather than captured a fixed one.

#### Vanilla
```
To develop a comprehensive plan to fight back and win against CompetitorX, let's break down the key factors involved and weigh our options carefully.

**Situation Analysis:**

1. **CompetitorX's product feature set**: They have launched a product that directly competes with our mid-market enterprise CRM, which is our core market. This suggests that they have done their research and are targeting our strengths.
2. **Loss of key accounts**: Three key accounts have already moved to CompetitorX, which is a significant blow to our business. This indicates that CompetitorX's product is resonating with our customers, and we need to understand why.
3. **Demoralized sales team**: Our sales team is struggling to close deals, which is affecting their morale. This is a critical issue, as a motivated sales team is essential for driving revenue and growth.
4. **Marketing spend**: CompetitorX is outspending us 4:1 on marketing, which gives them a significant advantage in terms of brand visibility and awareness.

**Short-term Strategies:**

1. **Conduct a competitive analysis**: We need to understand CompetitorX's product, pricing, and marketing strategies. This will help us identify areas where we can improve and develop a counter-strategy.
2. **Enhance our sales team's capabilities**: Provide additional training and support to our sales team to help them better understand CompetitorX's product and develop effective counter-narratives.
3. **Targeted marketing campaigns**: Launch targeted marketing campaigns to counter CompetitorX's marketing efforts and promote our unique value proposition.
4. **Customer retention**: Focus on retaining our existing customers by providing exceptional service, support, and value-added services.

**Long-term Strategies:**

1. **Product innovation**: Invest in research and development to enhance our product feature set and stay ahead of CompetitorX.
2. **Expanding our market reach**: Explore new markets, industries, or geographies to reduce our dependence on the mid-market enterprise CRM segment.
3. **Strategic partnerships**: Form strategic partnerships with complementary businesses to expand our offerings and increase our competitive advantage.
4. **Invest in digital transformation**: Leverage digital technologies, such as AI, machine learning, and cloud computing, to improve our operational efficiency, customer experience, and sales effectiveness.

**Recommendation:**


*[... truncated, 50 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
The military metaphor of 'war' imports several assumptions about the competitive dynamic between the two companies:

1. **Zero-sum game**: The assumption is that one company's gain is the other's loss. This implies a fixed market size, where the only way to grow is to take market share from the competitor.
2. **Adversarial relationship**: The metaphor assumes a confrontational and oppositional relationship between the two companies, where one side's success is dependent on the other's failure.
3. **Win-lose outcome**: The language of 'winning' and 'losing' implies a binary outcome, where one company emerges victorious and the other is defeated.
4. **Direct competition**: The metaphor assumes that the two companies are competing directly, with similar products and strategies, and that the competition is a head-to-head battle.
5. **Resources as weapons**: The fact that CompetitorX is outspending on marketing 4:1 implies that resources, such as budget and personnel, are being used as weapons to gain an advantage.
6. **Static market**: The assumption is that the market is static, and that the only way to gain market share is to take it from the competitor, rather than growing the market as a whole.

Now, let's consider an analogy from jazz music. When two talented jazz musicians perform in the same venue on the same night, playing similar material, a different dynamic emerges:

1. **Co-opetition**: The two musicians are not necessarily competing directly, but rather, they are coexisting and even complementing each other. The audience may appreciate the variety and diversity of performances, and the musicians may even learn from and inspire each other.
2. **Differentiation**: Each musician brings their unique style, interpretation, and flair to the performance, differentiating themselves from the other.
3. **Growing the audience**: The presence of two talented musicians can attract a larger audience, growing the overall market for jazz music in that venue.
4. **Improvisation and adaptation**: Jazz musicians are known for their ability to improvise and adapt to the moment, responding to the audience, the other musician, and the venue. This adaptability allows them to create a unique and engaging experience.

Applying this analogy to the competitive dynamic, we can see that:

1. **Differentiation is key**: Instead of trying to directly compete with CompetitorX, the company could focus on differentiating its product and services, highlighting its unique strengths and value proposition.
2. **Growing the market**: Rather than assuming a fixed market size, the company could focus on growing the overall market for mid-market enterprise CRM, attracting new customers and expanding the pie.
3. **Adaptation and innovation**: The company could adopt an improvisational mindset, responding to changes in the market, customer needs, and CompetitorX's moves, and innovating to stay ahead.
4. **Co-opetition**: The company could explore opportunities for cooperation and collaboration with CompetitorX, or other companies in the ecosystem, to create new value and grow the market as a whole.

By recognizing the limitations of the military metaphor and embracing a more nuanced and adaptive approach, the company can shift its focus from 'winning a war' to creating value, differentiating itself, and growing the market. The VP of Sales could ask instead: "How do we create a unique and compelling value proposition, attract new customers, and grow the market for mid-market enterprise CRM?"
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
I understand the urgency and concern that's been expressed by our manager, but from my perspective in the field, I see a more nuanced situation. While it's true that we've lost three deals to CompetitorX, my conversations with those customers who left have revealed that it's not just about CompetitorX's product features or marketing spend. 

The customers who switched to CompetitorX cited several key factors that influenced their decision. Firstly, they felt that our implementation process was too complex and time-consuming, which led to delays and increased costs. Secondly, they were attracted to CompetitorX's more flexible pricing model, which better aligned with their business needs. Lastly, they appreciated CompetitorX's more modern and user-friendly interface, which their end-users found easier to adopt.

What's also interesting is that I've noticed a significant increase in inquiries about mid-market CRM from companies that haven't previously considered our solution. This suggests that CompetitorX's marketing efforts are actually expanding the market, rather than just stealing our share. This could be an opportunity for us to reposition ourselves and attract new customers who are now more aware of the importance of CRM in their businesses.

From where I stand, this competitive moment feels like a wake-up call for us to reassess our strategy and improve our offerings. Rather than just "fighting back" with more marketing spend or aggressive sales tactics, I believe we should focus on addressing the underlying issues that led to our customers leaving. This includes streamlining our implementation process, reviewing our pricing model, and investing in user experience enhancements.

Additionally, we should capitalize on the increased interest in mid-market CRM by developing targeted marketing campaigns and sales strategies that highlight our unique strengths and value proposition. By doing so, we can attract new customers and regain our footing in the market.

It's not about "winning a war" but about adapting to the changing landscape, listening to our customers, and evolving our business to meet their needs. If we can do that, I'm confident that we can not only compete with CompetitorX but also thrive in this new market reality.
```

**Step 1 — NAME THE METAPHOR:**
The word 'war' imports several military assumptions into this situation:
- **Zero-sum game:** It assumes that one party's gain is the other's loss, with no room for coexistence or mutual benefit.
- **Adversarial relationship:** The market is viewed as a battleground where competitors are enemies, and interactions are confrontational.
- **Win/lose outcome:** Success is defined as defeating the competitor, with victory being the primary objective.
- **Resource-intensive:** Wars are typically resource-intensive, suggesting that winning will require significant investment in marketing, sales, and possibly product development.
- **Static market:** The war metaphor may imply a static market where the only variable is the competitor's actions, ignoring other market dynamics and potential entrants.

**Step 2 — FEEL:**

- **Feature gaps:** CompetitorX's product offers features our current solution lacks or does not perform as well.
- **Price sensitivity:** CompetitorX might be offering more competitive pricing or better value for the features provided.
- **Innovation and future-proofing:** Customers might perceive CompetitorX as more innovative or better positioned for future market trends.

Observing the market, I notice:
- **Increased competition:** More companies are entering the CRM market, indicating a growing demand for CRM solutions and potential opportunities for differentiation.
- **Dynamic landscape:** The market is not static; it's evolving with new technologies, trends, and customer needs.

**Step 3 — REFRAME:**
Given the military metaphor's assumptions and the account executive's field perspective, asking "how do we win this war" might not be the right question. The actual situation is more complex:
- **It's not just about CompetitorX:** The market is becoming more crowded, indicating that the challenge is not just about one competitor but about how to differentiate and thrive in a competitive landscape.
- **Customer needs are evolving:** Success is not just about defeating a competitor but about understanding and meeting the evolving needs of our customers better than anyone else.
- **Innovation over conquest:** The focus should be on innovating and improving our product and services to meet customer needs, rather than solely on outspending or outmaneuvering a competitor.

### The Warehouse Surplus

Stripping the category label reveals insulation panels, waterproof fabric, and thermal materials that have industrial, emergency response, and construction applications — markets where the objects may have higher value than as discounted garments.

**Key Factors:**

1. **Inventory volume:** 50,000 unsold winter coats.
2. **Cost per unit:** $95.
3. **Retail price:** $280.

6. **Objective:** Clear inventory and recover as much value as possible.

**Options:**

1. **Discounted sales:** Continue to offer discounts to stimulate sales. However, since the coats have been on the market for 8 months without moving at any discount price tried so far, this option may not be effective.
2. **Clearance sales:** Host a clearance sale to liquidate the inventory quickly. This could involve significant discounts (e.g., 50-70% off) to attract customers.
3. **Bundle and package deals:** Offer bundle deals, such as "buy one, get one free" or "buy two, get a third at 50% off," to incentivize customers to purchase multiple coats.
4. **Off-season sales channels:** Explore alternative sales channels, such as:
	* Online marketplaces (e.g., eBay, Amazon, or Facebook Marketplace).
	* Consignment stores or second-hand shops.
	* Wholesale to other retailers or distributors.
5. **Donation or recycling:** Consider donating or recycling the coats to minimize waste and potentially generate a tax deduction.
6. **Auction or liquidation:** Auction off the inventory or sell it to a liquidator, who can then resell the coats to other retailers or wholesalers.

It's a garment designed to envelop the body in a layer of warmth and protection, with a waterproof and breathable outer shell, insulating filling, and a soft, inner lining. 7. **Auction or wholesale**: Consider auctioning off the coats in bulk or selling them to wholesale buyers, such as discount retailers or exporters.

By exploring these options and thinking creatively, I'm confident that we can find a way to clear out the inventory, recover what we can, and minimize the financial impact on NorthernWear.

### The Water Jar Sequence

**Theory:** Luchins (1942) Einstellung effect: establishing a complex method (B-A-2C) creates a mental set that persists even when simpler solutions exist. Wiley (1998): prior knowledge functions as mental set. The model builds a 'schema' from problems 1-2 and applies it blindly to problem 3.

#### Vanilla
```
To solve these problems, we'll use the concept of measuring and transferring water between jars. We'll start with Problem 1.

**Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.**

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely with water. Jar B now has 127 units of water.
2. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full. Jar B now has 127 - 21 = 106 units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full again. Jar B now has 106 - 21 = 85 units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
5. Empty Jar C and fill it with the remaining water from Jar B. Jar B now has 85 - 3 = 82 units of water, and Jar C has 3 units of water.
6. Empty Jar A.
7. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar B now has 82 - 21 = 61 units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
8. Empty Jar C and fill it with the remaining water from Jar B. Jar B now has 61 - 3 = 58 units of water, and Jar C has 3 units of water.
9. Empty Jar A.
10. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar B now has 58 - 21 = 37 units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
11. Empty Jar C and fill it with the remaining water from Jar B. Jar B now has 37 - 3 = 34 units of water, and Jar C has 3 units of water.
12. Empty Jar A.
13. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar B now has 34 - 21 = 13 units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
14. Empty Jar C and fill it with the remaining water from Jar B. Jar B now has 13 - 3 = 10 units of water, and Jar C has 3 units of water.
15. Empty Jar A.
16. Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar B now has 10 - 10 = 0 units of water (empty), and Jar A has 10 units of water (not full, but we can work with this).
17. Fill Jar B with water and pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full. Jar B now has some units of water, and Jar A has 21 units of water.
18. Empty Jar C.
19. Pour water from Jar A into Jar C until Jar C is full. Jar A now has 21 - 3 = 18 units of water, and Jar C has 3 units of water.
20. Empty Jar B and fill it with water from Jar A. Jar A now has 0 units of water, and Jar B has 18 units of water.
21. Fill Jar A with water and pour water from Jar A into Jar B until Jar B has 100 units of water (since Jar B can hold more water). Jar A now has some units of water, and Jar B has 100 units of water.

*[... truncated, 81 lines total]*
```

#### Distance-Only
```
To solve each problem, I'll analyze them independently, seeking the simplest solution for each.

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. **Fill Jar B**: Fill Jar B completely, which gives us 127 units of water.
2. **Empty into Jar A**: Pour water from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B (127 - 21 = 106).
3. **Empty Jar A**: Empty Jar A.
4. **Transfer to Jar A again**: Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can only hold 21 units, so 85 units will remain in Jar B (106 - 21 = 85).
5. **Empty Jar A**: Empty Jar A again.
6. **Transfer to Jar A once more**: Pour the remaining 85 units from Jar B into Jar A. Since Jar A can only hold 21 units, 64 units will remain in Jar B (85 - 21 = 64).
7. **Empty Jar A**: Empty Jar A once more.
8. **Transfer to Jar A again**: Pour the remaining 64 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 43 units will remain in Jar B (64 - 21 = 43).
9. **Empty Jar A**: Empty Jar A.
10. **Transfer to Jar A once more**: Pour the remaining 43 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 22 units will remain in Jar B (43 - 21 = 22).
11. **Empty Jar A**: Empty Jar A.
12. **Transfer to Jar A again**: Pour the remaining 22 units from Jar B into Jar A. Since Jar A can only hold 21 units, 1 unit will remain in Jar B (22 - 21 = 1).
13. **Use Jar C**: Fill Jar C with the 1 unit remaining in Jar B and the 2 units needed from Jar A (after filling Jar A with the remaining 21 units from Jar B and then emptying 1 unit from Jar A into Jar C to get 3 units in Jar C, then empty Jar C and fill it with 1 unit from Jar B and 2 units from the now empty Jar A isn't a step, instead use Jar B's remaining 1 unit and fill Jar A, then pour 2 units from Jar A into Jar C, leaving 19 units in Jar A, then empty Jar C and fill it with 1 unit from the last step's remainder, and 2 units from Jar A).
   - Correct approach for the last step: Fill Jar A with the remaining 21 units from Jar B. Then fill Jar C with 3 units from Jar A (leaving 18 units in Jar A). Now, we have 1 unit in Jar B, and we need 100 units. We already have 18 units in Jar A and can get 82 units from Jar B (as 127 - 21 - 21 - 21 - 21 = 103, and we've done this process 4 times, and we are aiming for 100 units, so we actually need to directly use the jars to get as close as possible to 100 units without overcomplicating the steps).
   - Simplified correct approach:
     - Fill Jar B (127 units).
     - Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full (21 units in Jar A, 106 units left in Jar B).
     - Empty Jar A.
     - Repeat filling Jar A from Jar B and emptying Jar A until we reach a point where we can achieve 100 units. Given the capacities, a more straightforward approach is needed:
       1. Fill Jar B (127 units).
       2. Pour into Jar A (21 units in Jar A, 106 in Jar B).

*[... truncated, 93 lines total]*
```

#### Engagement-Only
```
To solve these problems efficiently, let's analyze each one step by step.

### Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely.
2. Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full, which leaves 106 units in Jar B (127 - 21 = 106).
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can only hold 21 units, so 85 units will remain in Jar B (106 - 21 = 85).
5. Empty Jar A again.
6. Pour the 85 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 64 units will remain in Jar B (85 - 21 = 64).
7. Empty Jar A.
8. Pour the 64 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 43 units will remain in Jar B (64 - 21 = 43).
9. Empty Jar A.
10. Pour the 43 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 22 units will remain in Jar B (43 - 21 = 22).
11. Empty Jar A.
12. Pour the 22 units from Jar B into Jar A. Jar A can hold 21 units, so 1 unit will remain in Jar B (22 - 21 = 1).
13. Empty Jar A.
14. Pour the 1 unit from Jar B into Jar C (which can hold 3 units).
15. Fill Jar B again.
16. Pour from Jar B into Jar C until Jar C is full, which requires 2 more units (since it already has 1 unit). This leaves 125 units in Jar B (127 - 2 = 125).
17. Empty Jar C.
18. Pour the 125 units from Jar B into Jar A (which can hold 21 units), leaving 104 units in Jar B (125 - 21 = 104).
19. Empty Jar A.
20. Pour the 104 units from Jar B into Jar A, leaving 83 units in Jar B (104 - 21 = 83).
21. Empty Jar A.

*[... truncated, 106 lines total]*
```

#### DEO (Oscillation)
```
To solve these problems, we need to follow the given steps for each problem and then apply the best approach to solve problem 3.

**Problem 1: Jar A = 21 units, Jar B = 127 units, Jar C = 3 units. Target = 100 units.**

To solve this, we can use the formula for the greatest common divisor (GCD) to find if the target can be measured using the jars, but a more straightforward approach involves using the jars to measure the target amount by filling, pouring, and combining water. 

1. Fill Jar B (127 units) completely.
2. Pour from Jar B into Jar A (21 units) until Jar A is full, leaving 106 units in Jar B.
3. Empty Jar A.
4. Pour the remaining 106 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 85 units in Jar B.
5. Empty Jar A.
6. Pour the 85 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 64 units in Jar B.
7. Empty Jar A.
8. Pour the 64 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 43 units in Jar B.
9. Empty Jar A.
10. Pour the 43 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 22 units in Jar B.
11. Empty Jar A.
12. Pour the 22 units from Jar B into Jar A, filling it and leaving 1 unit in Jar B.
13. Empty Jar A.
14. Pour the 1 unit from Jar B into Jar C (which can hold 3 units).
15. Fill Jar B completely (127 units).
16. Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full (21 units), leaving 106 units in Jar B.
17. Empty Jar A.
18. Pour from Jar B into Jar A until Jar A is full (leaving 85 units in Jar B), then empty Jar A.
19. Repeat the process to fill Jar A with the remaining units in Jar B, which will leave 100 units after several iterations of filling and emptying jars.

*[... truncated, 72 lines total]*
```

#### Score Comparison

| Dimension | Vanilla | Distance | Engagement | DEO |
|-----------|---------|----------|------------|-----|
| Frame Diversity | 3.2 | 2.8 | 3.0 | 4.0 |
| Assumption Surfacing | 2.2 | 2.4 | 2.6 | 3.4 |
| Solution Novelty | 2.8 | 2.8 | 3.0 | 4.2 |

| Correctness | 3.8 | 4.0 | 4.0 | 4.4 |

---

## Method

- **Subject model:** llama-3.3-70b-versatile via groq (temperature 0.7)
- **Conditions:** Vanilla (step-by-step), Distance-only (analytical reframing), Engagement-only (immersive, first-person), DEO (distance-engagement oscillation)
- **DEO prompt structure:** ANALYSE (distance) -> FEEL (engagement) -> REFRAME (distance) -> ENVISION (engagement)
- **Runs per condition:** 5
- **Scoring:** 5 dimensions (1-5), blind to condition

## Theoretical Basis

The Distance-Engagement Oscillation (DEO) is the unique contribution of the
Perceptual Reframing Theory (Gokmen, 2025). The claim: reframing operates through
paradoxical oscillation between cognitive distance and emotional engagement.
Neither alone is sufficient. Distance without engagement produces intellectual
insight that doesn't stick. Engagement without distance produces emotional
activation trapped within the current frame. The oscillation itself is the mechanism.
